colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background-color: #DDDDFF; color: black">Statistics (2019)</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><table class="infobox-subbox"><tbody><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Passengers</th><td class="infobox-data">8,964,242</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Passenger change 18\xe2\x80\x9319</th><td class="infobox-data"><img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" class="infobox-label">Movements change 18-19</th><td class="infobox-data"><img alt="Decrease" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/11px-Decrease2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Decrease" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/17px-Decrease2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Decrease2.svg/22px-Decrease2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" />4.8%</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr style="background-color: #eee"><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><div style="text-align: left;">Sources: UK <a href="/wiki/Aeronautical_Information_Publication" title="Aeronautical Information Publication">AIP</a> at <a href="/wiki/National_Air_Traffic_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="National Air Traffic Services">NATS</a><sup id="cite_ref-aip_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><br />Statistics from the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">UK Civil Aviation Authority</a><sup id="cite_ref-stats_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stats-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Bristol Airport</b> (<a href="/wiki/IATA_airport_code" title="IATA airport code">IATA</a>: <b>BRS</b>, <a href="/wiki/ICAO_airport_code" title="ICAO airport code">ICAO</a>: <b>EGGD</b>), at <a href="/wiki/Lulsgate_Plateau" title="Lulsgate Plateau">Lulsgate Bottom</a>, on the northern slopes of the <a href="/wiki/Mendip_Hills" title="Mendip Hills">Mendip Hills</a>, in <a href="/wiki/North_Somerset" title="North Somerset">North Somerset</a>, is the commercial <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airport</a> serving the city of <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a>, England, and the surrounding area. It is 7 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> (13&#160;km; 8.1&#160;mi) southwest of Bristol city centre.<sup id="cite_ref-aip_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aip-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Built on the site of a former <a href="/wiki/RAF" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF">RAF</a> airfield, it opened in 1957 as <b>Bristol (Lulsgate) Airport</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> replacing <a href="/wiki/Bristol_(Whitchurch)_Airport" title="Bristol (Whitchurch) Airport">Bristol (Whitchurch) Airport</a> as Bristol\'s municipal airport. From 1997 to 2010, it was known as <b>Bristol International Airport</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-bristolairpot_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bristolairpot-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> In 1997, a majority shareholding in the airport was sold to <a href="/wiki/FirstGroup" title="FirstGroup">FirstGroup</a>, and then in 2001 the airport was sold to a joint venture of <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Group" title="Macquarie Group">Macquarie Bank</a> and others. In September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Teachers%27_Pension_Plan" title="Ontario Teachers&#39; Pension Plan">Ontario Teachers\' Pension Plan</a> bought out Macquarie to become the sole owner.\n</p><p>In 2019, it was ranked the eighth <a href="/wiki/Busiest_airports_in_the_United_Kingdom_by_total_passenger_traffic" class="mw-redirect" title="Busiest airports in the United Kingdom by total passenger traffic">busiest airport</a> (overtaking <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow International Airport">Glasgow International Airport</a> from the previous year) in the United Kingdom, handling over 8.9 million passengers, a 3% increase compared with 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-caa2019_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caa2019-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> A passenger survey carried out in 2015 found that 32.5% of journeys using the airport started or ended in the city of Bristol, 9.6% in <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, 24.5% in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a> and 16.9% in <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airlines with operating bases at the airport include <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a>. The airport has a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Civil Aviation Authority (United Kingdom)">Civil Aviation Authority</a> Public Use Aerodrome Licence (number P432) that allows flights for the public transport of passengers and for flying instruction.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label aviation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Accidents_and_incidents"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Accidents and incidents</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#References"><span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg/220px-Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg/330px-Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg/440px-Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2132" data-file-height="1441" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cambrian_viscount_g-amon_in_1963_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Viscount" title="Vickers Viscount">Vickers Viscount</a> loads at the airport in 1963</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg/220px-Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg/330px-Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg/440px-Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2922" data-file-height="1798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carvair_and_ambassador_at_bristol_airport_1965_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aviation_Traders_ATL-98_Carvair" class="mw-redirect" title="Aviation Traders ATL-98 Carvair">Aviation Traders Carvair</a> and the tail of an <a href="/wiki/Airspeed_Ambassador" title="Airspeed Ambassador">Airspeed Ambassador</a> in 1965</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_airport">First airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: First airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bristol_(Whitchurch)_Airport" title="Bristol (Whitchurch) Airport">Bristol (Whitchurch) Airport</a></div>\n<p>In 1927, a group of local businessmen raised \xc2\xa36,000 through public subscription to start the Bristol and Wessex Aeroplane Club,<sup id="cite_ref-guide_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guide-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> a flying club initially based at <a href="/wiki/Filton_Aerodrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Filton Aerodrome">Filton Aerodrome</a>. In 1929, <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Bristol Corporation">Bristol Corporation</a> took up the club\'s proposal to develop farmland located at <a href="/wiki/Whitchurch,_Bristol" title="Whitchurch, Bristol">Whitchurch</a>, to the south of Bristol, into a municipal airport.<sup id="cite_ref-Wake1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wake1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> On its opening by <a href="/wiki/Prince_George,_Duke_of_Kent" title="Prince George, Duke of Kent">Prince George, Duke of Kent</a> in 1930, Bristol (Whitchurch) Airport was the third civil airport in the United Kingdom. Passenger numbers grew to 4,000 by 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-guide_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guide-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Whitchurch was the main civil airport remaining operational. The newly formed <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Airways_Corporation" title="British Overseas Airways Corporation">British Overseas Airways Corporation</a> (BOAC) was transferred to Whitchurch from <a href="/wiki/Croydon_Airport" title="Croydon Airport">Croydon Airport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heston_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Heston Airport">Heston Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> BOAC operated routes around the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and to neutral nations. The Bristol\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> route (Portugal was a neutral nation) had both British and German planes flying there) was operated by the Dutch airline <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Air_charter" title="Air charter">charter</a> to BOAC.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> even flew from Bristol.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="RAF_Lulsgate_Bottom">RAF Lulsgate Bottom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: RAF Lulsgate Bottom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 1940, No 10 Elementary Flying Training School at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Weston-super-Mare" title="RAF Weston-super-Mare">RAF Weston-super-Mare</a> established a Relief Landing Ground on 14 acres (5.7&#160;ha) at Broadfield Down by the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Lulsgate_Bottom" class="mw-redirect" title="Lulsgate Bottom">Lulsgate Bottom</a>, southwest of the city and north of <a href="/wiki/Redhill,_Somerset" title="Redhill, Somerset">Redhill</a> village. Being high, at 600&#160;ft (180&#160;m), the site had a poor weather record during <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">warm front</a> conditions, when it was often covered in low <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-berryman_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berryman-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, when this occurred the alternative airfields at Filton and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Pengam_Moors" title="RAF Pengam Moors">Cardiff</a> were usually clear and operational; and as Lulsgate was clear when the low-lying airfields were obscured by <a href="/wiki/Radiation_fog" class="mw-redirect" title="Radiation fog">radiation fog</a> in calm weather, the landing ground provided a useful alternative. Few facilities were constructed although pillboxes, defensive anti-aircraft guns and later two <a href="/wiki/Blister_hangar" title="Blister hangar">Blister hangars</a> were added.<sup id="cite_ref-berryman_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berryman-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In late 1940, a <a href="/wiki/Starfish_site" title="Starfish site">Starfish site</a> was set up<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> south of the village of Downside and just west of the airfield.<sup id="cite_ref-James13_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James13-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Its decoy fires attracted a large quantity of <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> high explosives and <a href="/wiki/Incendiaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Incendiaries">incendiaries</a> on the nights of 16 March, 3 April and 4 April 1941 during the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blitz" title="Bristol Blitz">Bristol Blitz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1941, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a> planned to use the airfield for an experimental unit, and after requisitioning land from several adjacent farms, contracted <a href="/wiki/George_Wimpey" title="George Wimpey">George Wimpey</a> and Company to begin work on 11 June 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-berryman_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berryman-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> However, its intended use soon changed into being a satellite airfield for the fighter squadrons based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Colerne" title="RAF Colerne">RAF Colerne</a>. Originally, the new airfield\'s name was to be RAF Broadfield Down.<sup id="cite_ref-Penny154_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penny154-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The airfield used a standard-issue <a href="/wiki/Class_A_airfield" title="Class A airfield">three-runway layout</a> resembling an A shape. The main, east-west runway was 3,891&#160;ft (1,186&#160;m) long, with a designated alignment of 28/10, and the others were 3,300&#160;ft (1,000&#160;m) aligned 21/03 and 3,294&#160;ft (1,004&#160;m) aligned 34/16.<sup id="cite_ref-James13_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James13-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The first aircraft to land was a Luftwaffe <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_88" title="Junkers Ju 88">Ju 88</a> at 06.20 on 24 July 1941. Returning from a raid, its crew had been deceived by the RAF electronic countermeasures radio beacon at <a href="/wiki/Lympsham" title="Lympsham">Lympsham</a>, which was re-radiating the signal from a Luftwaffe homing beacon at <a href="/wiki/Brest,_France" title="Brest, France">Brest</a>, France.<sup id="cite_ref-berryman_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-berryman-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1942, there was no longer a need for an additional fighter airfield. With its name changed to RAF Lulsgate Bottom,<sup id="cite_ref-Penny154_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penny154-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> the airfield was declared operational on 15 January 1942. The <a href="/wiki/Miles_Master" title="Miles Master">Miles Masters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airspeed_Oxford" title="Airspeed Oxford">Airspeed Oxfords</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hawker Hurricanes</a> of <a href="/wiki/No._286_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 286 Squadron RAF">No. 286 (AA Cooperation) Squadron</a> became resident, with the role of providing realistic exercises for ground anti-aircraft defences. However, as the site lacked some basic facilities, No. 286 moved to <a href="/wiki/RAF_Zeals" title="RAF Zeals">RAF Zeals</a> in May.<sup id="cite_ref-Chew76_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chew76-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> From 1 June 1942, the airfield was under No. 23 Group of <a href="/wiki/Flying_Training_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Flying Training Command">Flying Training Command</a>, and initially became a satellite airfield for No. 3 (Pilot) Advanced Flying Unit (3 (P)AFU), based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_South_Cerney" title="RAF South Cerney">RAF South Cerney</a>, flying Oxfords.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In March 1943, No. 1540 <a href="/wiki/Beam_approach_beacon_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Beam approach beacon system">Beam Approach</a> Training Flight (1540 BATF) was formed at Lulsgate, again flying Oxfords.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> On 27 September 1943, 3 (P)AFU left Lulsgate for <a href="/wiki/RAF_Southrop" title="RAF Southrop">RAF Southrop</a>, and was replaced on 1 October 1943 by No. 3 Flying Instructors School (3 FIS), which was previously headquartered at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hullavington" title="RAF Hullavington">RAF Hullavington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-James30_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James30-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> 3 FIS flew mostly Oxfords and some Masters.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1944, BOAC started to use the airfield for <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Dakota" class="mw-redirect" title="Douglas Dakota">Douglas Dakota</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_Liberator" class="mw-redirect" title="Consolidated Liberator">Consolidated Liberator</a> crew training,<sup id="cite_ref-James30_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James30-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and BOAC flights made use of it occasionally as an alternate airfield for Whitchurch,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and for topping-up fuel on the Bristol\xe2\x80\x93Lisbon route.<sup id="cite_ref-James30_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James30-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 6 February 1945, 1540 BATF left for <a href="/wiki/RAF_Weston_Zoyland" title="RAF Weston Zoyland">RAF Weston Zoyland</a>. On 18 July 1945, 3 FIS was absorbed into 7 FIS.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> With the war over, the RAF ceased training at Lulsgate on 15 April 1946, and the next month 7 FIS left the airfield and joined the <a href="/wiki/Central_Flying_School" title="Central Flying School">Central Flying School</a> at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Little_Rissington" title="RAF Little Rissington">RAF Little Rissington</a>. The RAF finally abandoned Lulsgate on 25 October 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lulsgate_Bottom_Airfield">Lulsgate Bottom Airfield</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lulsgate Bottom Airfield">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From 1948, the site was the home of the Bristol Gliding Club. In 1949 and 1950, the Bristol Motor Cycle and Light Car Club hosted motor races on a 2&#160;mi (3.2&#160;km) circuit known as <a href="/wiki/Lulsgate_Aerodrome" title="Lulsgate Aerodrome">Lulsgate Aerodrome</a>, but due to planning and noise issues moved in 1950 to a site that became known as <a href="/wiki/Castle_Combe_Circuit" title="Castle Combe Circuit">Castle Combe Circuit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chew76_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chew76-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Bristol_.28Lulsgate.29_Airport"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bristol_(Lulsgate)_Airport">Bristol (Lulsgate) Airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bristol (Lulsgate) Airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Whitchurch airport continued to be used after World War II, but the introduction of heavier post-war airliners made a runway extension highly desirable. However, this was difficult at Whitchurch, because of the nearby housing estates.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In June 1955, the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Transport_and_Civil_Aviation" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister of Transport and Civil Aviation">Minister of Transport and Civil Aviation</a> agreed to sell the Lulsgate airfield to Bristol Corporation, for the development of a new airport there.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Bristol Gliding Club moved out to <a href="/wiki/Nympsfield" title="Nympsfield">Nympsfield</a> in Gloucestershire.<sup id="cite_ref-Chew76_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chew76-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the purchase price of \xc2\xa355,000, the city spent a further \xc2\xa3200,000 by 1958 on building the <a href="/wiki/Airport_terminal" title="Airport terminal">terminal</a> and other development.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In mid-April 1957, all air traffic was transferred from Whitchurch to the new airport.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> With the name of Bristol (Lulsgate) Airport,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> it was officially opened on 1 May 1957 by <a href="/wiki/Princess_Marina,_Duchess_of_Kent" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Marina, Duchess of Kent">Princess Marina, Duchess of Kent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In the airport\'s first year it was used by 33,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Bristol and Wessex Aeroplane Club also moved to Lulsgate.<sup id="cite_ref-Chew76_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chew76-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1962, a new <a href="/wiki/Control_tower" class="mw-redirect" title="Control tower">control tower</a> was built,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and in 1969 the runway was lengthened and extensions were made to the terminal.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott92_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott92-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 1968 a new 5,000&#160;sq&#160;ft (460&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) cargo transit shed was constructed.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In 1974, the airline <a href="/wiki/Court_Line" title="Court Line">Court Line</a> collapsed, causing a fall in passenger numbers.\n</p><p>By 1980, although 17 <a href="/wiki/Charter_airline" class="mw-redirect" title="Charter airline">charter airlines</a> were operating from the airport,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> it was making a loss.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Les Wilson took over as managing director in that year, a position which he held until his death in a car crash in November 1995; much of the airport\'s subsequent strong recovery over that period has been attributed to him.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The airport moved back into profit in financial year 1981/82,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and by 1983/84 the profit was \xc2\xa30.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In 1984, an international departure lounge was added, with <a href="/wiki/Duty-free" class="mw-redirect" title="Duty-free">duty-free</a> shops and a 24-hour air-side bar.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Airports Act 1986 required every municipal airport with a turnover greater than \xc2\xa31M to be turned into a <a href="/wiki/Public_limited_company" title="Public limited company">public limited company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott86_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott86-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> On 1 April 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Bristol City Council transferred the operation and net assets of the airport to Bristol Airport plc. The council retained full ownership of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-mplan_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mplan-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> However, under the terms of the Act, as long as the local authority retained a majority shareholding there were restrictions on the ability of the company to raise finance for capital projects.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott86_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott86-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1988, the airport opened a new <a href="/wiki/Concourse" title="Concourse">concourse</a> area.<sup id="cite_ref-Elliott92_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elliott92-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, a <a href="/wiki/Planning_application" class="mw-redirect" title="Planning application">planning application</a> for a new terminal was approved.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> With other projects also planned, the council decided to sell a majority shareholding in the airport, so that the restrictions imposed by the Airports Act on raising the necessary finance could be removed.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcFirstBus_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcFirstBus-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bristol_International_Airport">Bristol International Airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Bristol International Airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg/170px-Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg/255px-Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg/340px-Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol.airport.tower.arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The control tower in 2003</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol_International_Airport,_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg/330px-Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="88" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg/495px-Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg/660px-Bristol_International_Airport%2C_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6957" data-file-height="1851" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bristol_International_Airport,_terminal_building_departure_area.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terminal building check-in area in 2008</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg/220px-Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg/330px-Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg/440px-Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Main_Terminal_Bristol_Airport_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1500069.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terminal frontage in 2009</div></div></div>\n<p>In mid-1997, the airport\'s name was changed to Bristol International Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In November 1997, the successful bidder for the purchase of a 51% stake in the airport company was revealed to be <a href="/wiki/FirstGroup" title="FirstGroup">FirstBus</a>. The remaining 49% was retained by the council. Work on the new terminal building had already started;<sup id="cite_ref-bbcFirstBus_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcFirstBus-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> it opened in March 2000, at a cost of \xc2\xa327M.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In 2000, passenger numbers exceeded two million for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> A new control tower was built and the <a href="/wiki/A38_road" title="A38 road">A38 road</a> was diverted to cater for the installation of a <a href="/wiki/Instrument_landing_system" title="Instrument landing system">Category 3 instrument landing system</a>; these projects were completed in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Chew76_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chew76-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2001, the airport was purchased for \xc2\xa3198M,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> by a joint venture of <a href="/wiki/Macquarie_Group" title="Macquarie Group">Macquarie Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cintra" title="Cintra">Cintra</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Ferrovial" title="Ferrovial">Ferrovial</a> group.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Ferrovial sold its 50% share to Macquarie in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Teachers%27_Pension_Plan" title="Ontario Teachers&#39; Pension Plan">Ontario Teachers\' Pension Plan</a> made two substantial share purchases, in 2002 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2001, the low-cost carrier <a href="/wiki/Go_Fly" class="mw-redirect" title="Go Fly">Go Fly</a> made Bristol Airport its second base after <a href="/wiki/Stansted_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Stansted Airport">Stansted</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-anna_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anna-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Passenger numbers passed through three million in 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> largely due to Go\'s arrival. <a href="/wiki/EasyJet" title="EasyJet">EasyJet</a> purchased Go in 2002,<sup id="cite_ref-anna_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anna-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> took over the base in 2003 and continued its rapid growth in destinations.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In May 2005, <a href="/wiki/Continental_Airlines" title="Continental Airlines">Continental Airlines</a> introduced a direct flight from Bristol to <a href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport" title="Newark Liberty International Airport">Newark</a> with <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757</a> aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> though this ceased in November 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-newarkcancel_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newarkcancel-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A new asphalt runway surface was laid between November 2006 and March 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-AAIB_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAIB-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> at a cost of \xc2\xa317M.<sup id="cite_ref-reopens_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reopens-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Within this period, on 29 December and 3 January, there were four incidents of reduced braking action in wet conditions on the temporary surface, including two in which aircraft left the runway.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc9_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc9-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> From 5 January, ten airlines, led by EasyJet, cancelled or diverted their Bristol flights. The airport closed the runway on 7 January to cut grooves into the surface to improve water runoff, and flights resumed the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-reopens_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reopens-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a> established a base at the airport in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, passenger numbers reached six million.<sup id="cite_ref-history_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bristol_Airport">Bristol Airport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bristol Airport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In March 2010, the airport was rebranded as Bristol Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The airport gained a new logo, said by the airport\'s owners to represent \'people\', \'place\' and \'region\'; and a new slogan, "Amazing journeys start here".<sup id="cite_ref-bristolairpot_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bristolairpot-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bristol Airport does not operate any <a href="/wiki/Jet_bridge" title="Jet bridge">jetways</a>, so aircraft have to park on the <a href="/wiki/Airport_apron" title="Airport apron">apron</a> and passengers either walk out to their flights or are carried by bus. May 2010 saw the opening of a 450&#160;m (1,480&#160;ft)<sup id="cite_ref-2010walkway_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010walkway-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> walkway to the west of the terminal building,<sup id="cite_ref-2013walkway_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2013walkway-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> connecting it to eight new pre-boarding zones, at a cost of \xc2\xa38M, to reduce the need for buses.<sup id="cite_ref-2010walkway_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2010walkway-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, <a href="/wiki/BMI_Regional" class="mw-redirect" title="BMI Regional">BMI Regional</a> established a base at the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, the airline added routes to German and Italian <a href="/wiki/Hub_airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Hub airport">hub airports</a>, aimed at business travellers.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2014, <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Teachers%27_Pension_Plan" title="Ontario Teachers&#39; Pension Plan">Ontario Teachers\' Pension Plan</a> agreed to buy Macquarie\'s remaining 50% stake in the airport, thereby gaining 100% ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 November 2020, <a href="/wiki/Jet2.com" title="Jet2.com">Jet2.com</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Jet2holidays" title="Jet2holidays">Jet2holidays</a> announced that they would open their tenth UK base at Bristol Airport on 1 April 2021, operating to 33 destinations; however as a result of the ongoing coronavirus travel restrictions, the base opening and flights were delayed until 1 July 2021. <sup id="cite_ref-auto1_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion">Expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In response to the UK Governments\'s 2003 <a href="/wiki/White_Paper" class="mw-redirect" title="White Paper">White Paper</a> <i>The Future of Air Transport</i>, the airport published a Master Plan for expansion over the period 2006\xe2\x80\x932030.<sup id="cite_ref-mplan_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mplan-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In October 2007, the airport announced that it would delay the planning application until the middle of 2008 to give it time to complete research on the airport\'s effect on the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Development_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="World Development Movement">World Development Movement</a> claimed that flights from the airport generated the same amount of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as the nation of Malawi.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> A campaign against the plan was led by Stop Bristol Airport Expansion, supported by Bristol <a href="/wiki/Friends_of_the_Earth" title="Friends of the Earth">Friends of the Earth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_to_Protect_Rural_England" title="Campaign to Protect Rural England">Campaign to Protect Rural England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The application was eventually submitted in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-submitted_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-submitted-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The \xc2\xa3150M plan,<sup id="cite_ref-hurdle_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hurdle-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> designed to facilitate growth in annual passenger numbers to 10 million,<sup id="cite_ref-submitted_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-submitted-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> was approved by <a href="/wiki/North_Somerset_Council" title="North Somerset Council">North Somerset Council</a> in 2010 and by the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Communities_and_Local_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Communities and Local Government">Secretary of State for Communities and Local Government</a> later the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-hurdle_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hurdle-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In October 2011, Stop Bristol Airport Expansion lost its legal challenge to the plan.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc1_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The expansion was to occur in stages, spread over 30 construction projects.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc2_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc2-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Plans included a doubling of passenger terminal floorspace, new piers and aircraft parking stands, extensions to the apron, multi-storey car parking and a public transport interchange.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The first project was completed in June 2012, with the opening of three new aircraft stands.<sup id="cite_ref-wm_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wm-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In July 2014, a 3,880&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (41,800&#160;sq&#160;ft), \xc2\xa36.5M walkway connected to the centre of the terminal was opened, providing four more pre-boarding zones and allowing the use of <a href="/wiki/Jetways" class="mw-redirect" title="Jetways">jetways</a>, including for <a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">wide-body aircraft</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2014walkway_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014walkway-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In July 2015, the airport opened an \xc2\xa38.6M eastward extension of the terminal, having a larger departure lounge and an outdoor terrace. Construction of another terminal extension started immediately, to the west and costing \xc2\xa324M.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The first phases of the 9,000-square-metre (97,000&#160;sq&#160;ft) western extension, which opened in summer 2016, provided a new security search area for departures, with 12 security lanes including a fast track zone. New arrivals facilities within the extension, including <a href="/wiki/Baggage_reclaim" title="Baggage reclaim">baggage reclaim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Customs" title="Customs">customs</a>, were scheduled to open later in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In October 2016, the airport announced that a further project, an enlargement of the immigration hall, will complete in 2017. These were completed and opened to the public in April 2017, enabling an increase in the number of <a href="/wiki/Passport" title="Passport">passport</a> control points from 10 to 17, of which 10 are <a href="/wiki/EPassport_gates" title="EPassport gates">ePassport gates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A planning application for an on-site 251-room hotel was approved separately in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In February 2014, a planning application was submitted for a revision to the previously approved design, with a 201-room hotel to be built initially, followed later by a 50-room addition. The airport stated that among the UK\'s busiest 16 airports, only Bristol lacked an on-site hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In February 2015, the airport announced that the 201-room hotel would be completed in 2016, and will be operated as a <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Inn" class="mw-redirect" title="Hampton Inn">Hampton by Hilton</a>. It opened for bookings in January 2017. It was funded, built and is owned, by a Chinese company,<sup id="cite_ref-hotel1_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hotel1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China_International_Marine_Containers" title="China International Marine Containers">CIMC</a> Modular Building Systems,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> who shipped <a href="/wiki/Prefabricated" class="mw-redirect" title="Prefabricated">prefabricated</a> modules for its construction from China.<sup id="cite_ref-hotel1_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hotel1-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Work on a \xc2\xa39.5M multi-storey car park began in November 2017, following a \xc2\xa32.5M upgrade to the customer reception centre in the silver zone car park.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The new car park opened in May 2018. \n</p><p>Bristol Airport set up a consultation which ran between 16 November 2017 and 26 January 2018 and sought opinions on the airport\'s priorities and initial concepts for developing the airport. \n</p><p>In 2018 the airport applied to extend the airport to allow a growth in passenger numbers to 12 million. The plan involved enlarging the passenger terminus and plane taxiways. It also planned to add parking for 3,000 more cars, much of it on greenbelt land. There was much resistance to the plan with 84% of North Somerset residents who commented rejecting the expansion plan. Bristol Airport Action Network (BAAN), a network of organisations including Extinction Rebellion, North Somerset Parishes, Stop Airport Expansion, Friends of the Earth &amp; Greenpeace organised a huge resistance. This culminated in North Somerset council voting 18 to 7 to reject the plan on 10 February 2020. It said the detrimental effect on the area and the wider impact on the environment outweighed the narrower benefits to airport expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The airport lodged an appeal, and a four-week public inquiry is due to start in July 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-bp-20210104_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bp-20210104-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Airlines_and_destinations">Airlines and destinations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Airlines and destinations">edit</a><span class="mw-headline" id="Runway">Runway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Runway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Bristol Airport has one runway designated 09/27. As the prevailing wind is from the southwest, runway 27 (the westerly direction) is used about 70% of the time. The airport has one of the shortest international airport runways in the country at just 2,011&#160;m (6,598&#160;ft) in length, with runway 27 having a threshold displacement of 140m(510ft). Despite the short runway length, the airfield is able to accommodate aircraft as large as the Boeing 787 Dreamliner and <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mplan_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mplan-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground_transport">Ground transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ground transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg/220px-Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg/330px-Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg/440px-Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Temple_Meads_Bristol_Airport_Flyer_23021.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Bristol Airport Flyer picking up at <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Temple_Meads_railway_station" title="Bristol Temple Meads railway station">Bristol Temple Meads railway station</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Bristol Airport is located on the <a href="/wiki/A38_road" title="A38 road">A38</a>, 8&#160;mi (13&#160;km) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Bristol_city_centre" title="Bristol city centre">Bristol city centre</a>. The airport is signposted from the <a href="/wiki/M5_motorway" title="M5 motorway">M5 motorway</a>, from junction 22 when approaching from the south and junction 18 when approaching from the north. Neither gives quick access to the airport, a fact which was recognised by the Greater Bristol Strategic Transport Study.<sup id="cite_ref-mplan_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mplan-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In November 2013, Bristol and North Somerset councils approved a planning application for the South Bristol Link Road,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> which provides a link from the A38 northwards to the <a href="/wiki/A370_road" title="A370 road">A370</a> at <a href="/wiki/Long_Ashton" title="Long Ashton">Long Ashton</a>, giving the airport an improved connection to the M5, and a link from the A38 southwards to <a href="/wiki/Hengrove_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="Hengrove Park">Hengrove Park</a>, connecting to the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Ring_Road" class="mw-redirect" title="Bristol Ring Road">Bristol Ring Road</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The South Bristol Link, part of the <a href="/wiki/MetroBus_(Bristol)" title="MetroBus (Bristol)">MetroBus</a> rapid transit route,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> was opened in January 2017 and is expected to provide a reduction in journey times to the airport for both bus passengers and car drivers.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Bristol Airport Flyer bus service links the airport to <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Temple_Meads_railway_station" title="Bristol Temple Meads railway station">Bristol Temple Meads railway station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol_bus_station" title="Bristol bus station">Bristol bus station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The service, numbered A1, is operated by <a href="/wiki/First_West_of_England" title="First West of England">First West of England</a> on behalf of Bristol Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The same company operated the A2 service from/to <a href="/wiki/Weston-super-Mare" title="Weston-super-Mare">Weston-super-Mare</a>, and the A3 on a more direct route to/from <a href="/wiki/Weston-super-Mare_railway_station" title="Weston-super-Mare railway station">Weston-super-Mare railway station</a>. The A4 \'Air Decker\' service operated by <a href="/wiki/Bath_Bus_Company" title="Bath Bus Company">Bath Bus Company</a> links the airport with southern suburbs of Bristol, <a href="/wiki/Keynsham" title="Keynsham">Keynsham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Service A2 now only operates as far as Bedminster.  The \'South West Falcon\' service operated by <a href="/wiki/Stagecoach_South_West" title="Stagecoach South West">Stagecoach South West</a> runs between Bristol and <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>, via the airport and <a href="/wiki/Bridgwater" title="Bridgwater">Bridgwater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taunton" title="Taunton">Taunton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_rail_link">Proposed rail link</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Proposed rail link">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Airport_Rail_Link" title="Bristol Airport Rail Link">Bristol Airport Rail Link</a></div>\n<p>In July 2016, the airport\'s chief executive officer Robert Sinclair discussed the possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Airport_Rail_Link" title="Bristol Airport Rail Link">rail link</a> to the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The West of England LEP subsequently announced their application to the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a>\'s Large Local Major Transport Schemes fund for the "South West Bristol Economic Link" \xe2\x80\x93 a strategy designed to address "poor connectivity between North Somerset, Bristol Airport and Bristol", which includes new road links as well as light or heavy rail opportunities.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\nBy 2019, this proposal had been expanded as a <a href="/wiki/Light_rail_in_Bristol" title="Light rail in Bristol">mass transit</a> line with potential for underground sections.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_aviation">General aviation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: General aviation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg/220px-Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg/330px-Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg/440px-Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2505" data-file-height="1597" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cessna_525a_citationjet_cj2_g-ocjz_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Centreline Air Charter <a href="/wiki/Cessna_Citation_CJ2" class="mw-redirect" title="Cessna Citation CJ2">Cessna Citation CJ2</a> taking off at Bristol Airport.</div></div></div>\n<p>Bristol Airport is a <a href="/wiki/General_aviation" title="General aviation">general aviation</a> (GA) centre. In 2006, the GA terminal was relocated from the north side next to the control tower to a purpose-built facility on the south east corner of the field. Handling for visiting executive GA aircraft is managed by Bristol Flying Centre, which also provides engineering services and operates a fleet of business jets trading as Centreline Air Charter. Handling for light GA aircraft is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Bristol_and_Wessex_Aeroplane_Club" title="Bristol and Wessex Aeroplane Club">Bristol and Wessex Aeroplane Club</a>.\n</p><p>In 2012, Bristol Flying Centre doubled the size of its terminal,<sup id="cite_ref-Eva_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eva-108">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> to 6,500&#160;sq&#160;ft (600&#160;m<sup>2</sup>), with self-contained security facilities and two new passenger lounges.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Following the closure of <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Filton_Airport" title="Bristol Filton Airport">Bristol Filton Airport</a> at the end of 2012, Bristol Flying Centre gained <a href="/wiki/Fixed-base_operator" title="Fixed-base operator">fixed-base operator</a> traffic such as the corporate shuttle for <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a>, flying to <a href="/wiki/Toulouse%E2%80%93Blagnac_Airport" title="Toulouse\xe2\x80\x93Blagnac Airport">Toulouse</a>, and the shuttle for <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems" title="BAE Systems">BAE Systems</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sarsfield_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarsfield-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In July 2013, the Department for Transport gave approval for Bristol Flying Centre to handle charter flights directly, without needing to clear through the main airport terminal.<sup id="cite_ref-Sarsfield_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sarsfield-110">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, a new building called The Bristol Flying School was constructed to re-house the Bristol &amp; Wessex Aeroplane Club and to contain a flying school operated by Aeros Flight Training, which formerly operated at Filton Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">Accidents and incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bristol_Airport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Accidents and incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>On 19 January 1970, <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Viscount" title="Vickers Viscount">Vickers Viscount</a> G-AMOA of <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_Airways" title="Cambrian Airways">Cambrian Airways</a> was damaged beyond economic repair in a heavy landing.<sup id="cite_ref-ASN19011970_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASN19011970-112">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 3 October 2010, a <a href="/wiki/TUI_Airways" title="TUI Airways">Thomson Airways</a> <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767</a> aircraft, registration G-OOBK, landed heavily on runway 09 in severe weather conditions, reduced visibility and turbulence. The flight number 519 was from <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn_International_Airport" title="Canc\xc3\xban International Airport">Canc\xc3\xban Airport</a> in Mexico and carried 258 passengers and 12 crew members. No injuries were reported. During approach, the commander decided to perform a manual landing as weather reported at Bristol was not consistent with that presented to them at their briefing. Both the commander and co-pilot were thrown forward during the touchdown, and this resulted in the commander pushing the control column forward, to a nose down position. The aircraft then rapidly pitched up and down, before eventually settling on the landing gear. None of the pilots had recent or regular experience of landings on runway 09, and they only operated to Bristol Airport approximately twice a year.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 December 2017, a <a href="/wiki/BMI_Regional" class="mw-redirect" title="BMI Regional">BMI Regional</a> <a href="/wiki/Embraer_ERJ_145" class="mw-redirect" title="Embraer ERJ 145">Embraer ERJ145</a> aircraft, registration G-CKAG, exited the runway and became grounded after landing at 11:35. The flight was from Frankfurt and was carrying 22 passengers and three crew. No injuries were reported. The parking brake had been applied instead of the speed brakes before landing. The aircraft touched down on runway 27, the crew lost control of the aircraft and the aircraft exited the runway and entered a grass zone to the left, crossing taxiway Hotel at speed, causing the main landing gear tyres to burst. The aircraft came to rest in the grass shortly after. The aircraft was towed to the gate some 14 hours later. The incident resulted in several flights being diverted to other airports on what was called "Frantic Friday" as holidaymakers and families travelled for the Christmas period.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output
